<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This collection, the Max S. Baucus Papers, represents the material sum of Max
Baucus’s political career, from his work at the Montana Constitutional Convention to
his appointment as the United States Ambassador to China. It includes manuscript
materials, artifacts and memorabilia, microfilm, audiovisual material, photographs,
and electronic records produced throughout Baucus’s time in the Montana House of
Representatives, the United States House of Representatives, the Senate, and
ambassadorial service. These records document policy and legislation development,
constituent services, Baucus’s committee service, political campaigns, his
membership in the Democratic Party, and office administration. Particularly
well-represented are Senate legislative materials, as well as constituent
correspondence from his time in both the House and the Senate.</p>
<p>The collection also includes some material from Baucus’s personal life which provides
a small snapshot of his daily and social activities throughout his time in public
service. This material primarily takes the form of photographs, ephemera,
miscellaneous correspondence and holiday cards, invitations, and notebooks.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Materials in this series provide insight into the personal and family history
of Max Baucus. They largely pertain to Baucus’s life outside of politics,
though his personal and political lives inevitably overlapped as a career
elected official.</p>
<arrangement encodinganalog="351">
<p>This series is divided into two sub-series: Personal and Electronic
Files.</p>
</arrangement>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries represents the physical portion of this series, and
contains invitations and materials related to various social events on
Capitol Hill which Baucus attended, including Presidential inaugurations
and White House events. It also contains a number of appointment books
and wallet diaries that provide a window into Baucus’s personal
schedule, in contrast to official scheduling material produced by
staffers. A nearly complete run of Baucus family Christmas cards is also
included, several of which feature artwork created by Baucus’s second
wife, Wanda Minge.</p>

<p>Correspondence found within this series is largely comprised of letters
marked ‘Administration-Personal’ by office staff, though there are
letters relating to specific incidents in Baucus’s life. The series also
includes personal notes and notebooks kept by Baucus throughout his
career, containing notes and observations taken during meetings,
hearings, and other events, reflections on various topical issues, draft
ideas for speeches, and campaign strategies. Many of these are undated.
The series likewise contains a number of writings and publications
authored by Baucus, including op-eds and two peer-reviewed papers. Also
notable is a pocket Constitution given to Baucus by Senator Robert Byrd
in 1995, which he carried with him throughout the remainder of his
Senate career.</p>

<p>While largely comprised of material focusing on Baucus’s personal life,
the series does contain some records relating to his family—chiefly
obituaries following the death of Baucus’s stepfather, John Baucus, and
correspondence between Jean Baucus and Ruth Chambers. Chambers was the
sister of Max Baucus’s biological father, Stephen Enke, and the
correspondence took place between Jean Baucus and Chambers following
Enke’s death in 1974. The series also contains a Baucus family tree and
family recipes.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains essays and op-eds authored by Baucus, as well as
a 1983 New York City Marathon athlete profile sheet. Files have been
renamed for clarity by the processing archivist.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s time in the Montana
House of Representatives. It includes press files, bills which were
sponsored or cosponsored by Baucus, voting records, and subject files.
Subject files constitute the majority of this series, and consist of
documents created and kept by Baucus and his staff during the course of
their legislative work. Materials contained within subject files include
correspondence, research materials, memos, notes, reports, and other related
items reflecting the pertinent political and legislative issues of the time.
Staffers assigned subjects according to a topical index. This index helped
to organize many administrative functions, including legislative research,
the tracking of constituent opinion, the compilation of Baucus’s legislative
achievements, and responses to constituent concerns.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains administrative records from Max Baucus’s time in the
U.S. House of Representatives. Collectively they provide a picture of the
day-to-day operations within Baucus’s Washington, D.C. and state offices,
reflecting the wide array of activities which take place within
Congressional offices.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains materials relating to trip planning, office
equipment and supply purchases, parking, management of office finances,
staff training and management, and administrative correspondence. Also
included are successful academy appointments, administrative
correspondence, and staff files generated by Baucus’s first Chief of
Staff, Steve Browning, who held the position from 1975-1982.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries is comprised of schedules outlining Baucus’s daily agenda
in great detail, with to-the-minute times, locations, and background
information. These files reflect the busy day-to-day schedule of a
member of Congress, as well as the level of work and planning required
by staffers to keep office operations running smoothly.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">

Representatives, largely centering on event scheduling, White House
tours, and thank-you notes. These files were originally labeled
incorrectly as memos and located elsewhere in the collection, and have
since been concentrated in one folder. Original file names have been
retained.</p>

while serving as a U.S. Representative. In the case of untitled
speeches, file names describe the event or group to which Baucus

<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains constituent correspondence sent to Max Baucus during his
time in the U.S. House of Representatives. Reflecting a variety of topical
and legislative issues of the day, these letters provide a broad overview of
Montanans’ concerns in mid- to late 1970s America. Environment and
agriculture were two primary topics of correspondence, with many letters
focusing on grazing, agricultural subsidies, proposed legislation, and
wilderness. Central to environmental issues at the time was the passage of
the Montana Wilderness Study Act (S. 393), introduced by Senator Lee
Metcalf, which became law in 1977 and was to have profound effects on
Montana’s land and its residents. Other common areas of concern included
Panama Canal negotiations, postal matters, the Equal Rights Amendment, gun
control, labor-related legislation, and the Milwaukee Railroad bankruptcy.
The majority of files are arranged alphabetically by subject, which were
assigned in accordance with an indexing system utilized in Baucus’s office
to organize, code and track constituent mail and other documents. This
series does not contain a name file.</p>

<p>Also contained within this series are form letters, mass mailings, and yellow
files. Form letters were drafted by staffers in response to issues which
generated large quantities of constituent mail—these summarized Baucus’s
position and eliminated the need to create a unique response for each
constituent. Mass mailings are mailings, often in the form of newsletters,
sent to constituents on a regular or semi-regular basis. These provided
updates on pending legislation, Baucus’s legislative and political
activities, information on upcoming events, and sometimes included
questionnaires and surveys. Yellow files consist of responses to
constituents printed on yellow carbon paper, which are arranged
alphabetically by constituent last name. This series also includes 9 reels
of microfilm, containing scans of constituent correspondence from 1976.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains legislative materials relating to Max Baucus’s time in
the U.S. House of Representatives, which present a clear view of the
legislative process from start to finish. These files outline the scope and
extent of Baucus’s legislative work throughout his first two terms in
Congress, generated and gathered by both Baucus and his staffers. Such work
included policy research and development, correspondence and collaboration
with members of Congress and other stakeholders, Committee hearings and
deliberations, gathering and collating data, and drafting and editing
legislation.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries includes bills and resolutions which Baucus sponsored or
co-sponsored.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains floor statements, prepared remarks delivered by
Baucus on the House floor.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains material profiling Baucus’s collaborative and
individual legislative work and viewpoints. It contains legislative
correspondence, typically consisting of correspondence between Baucus
and other members of Congress intended to generate support for or
opposition to legislation, initiatives, and issues. VIP
correspondence—correspondence with well-known or prominent figures—is
also included, as well as correspondence with the standing U.S.
President. Also included are hearing statements, prepared remarks
delivered by Baucus in Committee hearings, and voting records from the
94th and 95th Congresses.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains materials generated by Baucus’s Committee service
during his time in the House of Representatives. Baucus served on the
House Committee on Appropriations for the entirety of his two terms in
the House, and the majority of materials contained within this subseries
relate to Committee work undertaken by Baucus and his staffers relating
to investigations, hearings, legislation, nominations, and other
projects. Also included in this subseries are committee assignment
materials and files relating to the Committee for a More Active and
Effective Congress, a liberal political action committee founded by
Eleanor Roosevelt.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries is comprised of records which outline all aspects of
legislative research, development, and implementation. File types
include, but are not limited to, memos, correspondence, reports,
publications, legal briefs, invitations, and legislative drafts. Subject
files give a clear indication of the predominant issues which directed
Baucus and his staffers’ work. Rural issues—particularly post offices
and rural health—ranked high on the list, in addition to President
Carter’s zero-based budgeting mechanism for government planning.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains scanned Dear Colleague letters, floor statements,
hearing statements, and memos. The analog versions of these documents,
along with many other types of documents produced by Baucus’s office,
were scanned by staffers and converted to digital formats for storage in
the office’s document management system, OnBase. Many OnBase files were
duplicated or scattered during the transfer process from Baucus’s office
to the University of Montana, and the majority of the files in this
subseries have subsequently been rearranged by category and renamed to
clarify their content, due to a lack of adequate original description.
Dear Colleague letters and memos files reflect only a small portion of
their analog counterparts, whereas floor and hearing statements files
represent a near-complete replication of their analog counterparts.
While it is likely that many of the files in this subseries are
reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations
have not been fully established.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains administrative records from Max Baucus’s time in the
U.S. Senate, relating specifically to Washington, D.C. and district office
operations. Such records reveal the wide range of activities that regularly
occur in a Congressional office, particularly those related to legislative
research, scheduling, and handling the large volume of constituent mail.
They also point to the high turnover of legislative staffers and the fast
pace of legislative activity, as well as the subsequent need for detailed
training materials and a high level of communication.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains materials reflecting the administrative capacity
of a congressional office, and include a number of academy appointments,
requests for appointment to military service academies that were
ultimately successful. These files are not exhaustive; records of
academy appointees are maintained by the academies themselves. Also
contained within this subseries are a run of ‘Thank you Good Letters’,
letters sent to Max Baucus expressing appreciation for speaking
engagements, constituent assistance, support of various issues, and
other services provided. Such letters were identified and retained by
staffers for campaign and legislative record purposes. Friendly File
cards likewise record constituents willing—or potentially willing—to
assist in fundraising, canvassing, volunteering, and other supportive
efforts. Additional administrative files include mail reports, indexes
and filing keys, conference-planning materials, memos, invitations,
staff training manuals and guides, and trip files. In addition to
scheduling materials, trip files contain a myriad of documents including
memos, background information on people and places, and drafts of
prepared remarks. Of particular note in this subseries are those records
pertaining to the Montana Economic Development Summit, a recurring event
focusing on Montana jobs and the economy established by Max Baucus in
2000.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries, while similarly reflecting the administrative functions
of a Congressional office, is comprised of schedules and related
documents which outline Baucus’s daily agendas. These documents provide
great insight into the day-to-day activities of a member of Congress, as
well as the high level of planning and meticulous attention to detail
required by staffers to plan activities and maintain adherence to
scheduled activities. They include specific times, locations,
transportation information, background information on both issues and
people, and other useful contextual information.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains born-digital administrative records from Baucus’s
time in the Senate, records which were created in an electronic format.
These files act as a foil to analog administrative records, serving as
the digital footprint of administrative functions within Baucus’s
office. In addition to such day-to-day administrative material as
calendars, forms, call reports, and manuals, this subseries also
contains indexes of paper records sent to National Records Centers in
Denver, Colorado and Suitland, Maryland for preservation. Also included
are inventories of records which were created in preparation for their
shipment to the University of Montana.</p>

<p>Files from Baucus’s Senate website, www.baucus.senate.gov, have not been
retained. Archived iterations of the website between October 13, 1999
and April 4, 2014 can be found on the Internet Archive’s WayBack Machine
at https://web.archive.org/web/*/http://www.baucus.senate.gov/.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains press materials produced during Max Baucus’s time in the
U.S. Senate, providing insights into his communication style, public
persona, and representation in local and national press. They also reveal
the ways in which Baucus connected with his constituents, highlighting
prioritized topical issues and demographics.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Staffers regularly collected news clippings that mentioned or featured
Max Baucus, and those contained within this subseries document the
entirety of his career in the Senate. Scattered throughout are Max
Baucus Reports, short legislative updates and topical pieces that were
written by Baucus and published in various Montana newspapers between
1979 and 1984.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries consists of prepared statements released by Max Baucus
regarding legislation, current events, sponsored and cosponsored bills,
and other political and legislative issues of the day. It also contains
news headlines and summaries from Montana newspapers, which were filed
with some early office press releases. Beginning in March of 1993 press
releases were filed individually, with topics typed on individual folder
labels. While press releases are listed here by date, a list of these
topics is available upon request.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>It is possible to trace Max Baucus’s growing national exposure and
expertise on particular topics through this complete run of speeches and
public remarks given during his time in the Senate. Baucus gave speeches
to some groups on a regular basis, casting light on one of the ways in
which he maintained connections with important components of his base.
These included groups and events like the Montana Graingrowers
Association, the AFL-CIO, the Montana Cattlemen’s Association, the Wool
Growers Convention, the Montana Democratic Party Mansfield-Metcalf
Dinner, and high school and college commencements. We can also trace his
growing proficiency in Asian foreign affairs through the many speeches
relating to Vietnam, Korea, Japan, and especially China, focusing
particularly on trade, economic and human rights issues. For those
speeches which are untitled, file names describe the group(s) to which
Baucus spoke.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains preparatory and administrative press-related
materials, which include memos, interview preparations, and an indexed
directory to important speeches.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains press clips, press releases, and speeches
spanning Baucus’s Senate career. Speeches and some press releases have
been renamed to clarify file content. While it is likely that the
majority of these files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this
series, exact correlations have not been fully established. The
following speech files constitute only a portion of the files contained
within this subseries. A full list of files is available upon
request.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains constituent correspondence generated and received
throughout Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. Senate. Consisting of
correspondence generated by constituents, staffers, and Baucus himself, the
material in this series weaves a 35-year narrative of constituent concerns,
pressing topical issues, support for and opposition to legislation, and,
more broadly, the symbiotic relationship between Senator Baucus and his
constituents.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Constituent Correspondence contains correspondence between Montana
constituents and Max Baucus’s offices. Congressional offices undergo
high staff turnover, resulting in ever-evolving records management
systems and organizational schema. These evolutions are commonly
reflected in constituent correspondence management, given the large
volume of material, and are also affected by changes in technology. Such
was the case in Baucus’s Congressional offices, particularly with the
advent of in-house Senate computing technology in the mid-1980s.
Constituent correspondence organizational schemas have thus been pieced
together from office manuals, filing guides and computer code lists.
This subseries does not contain a name file.</p>

<p>Several unique categories of correspondence can be found in the first
fifteen boxes: ‘Dear Montana’, Form Letters, Mass Mailings, and Robo
Letters. There is no clear distinction between ‘Dear Montana’
correspondence and other constituent correspondence. Form Letters
consist of letters drafted by staffers in response to issues which had
generated large quantities of constituent mail, summarizing Baucus’s
position and eliminating the need to craft a unique response to each
constituent. Similarly, Mass Mailings are mailings--often in the form of
newsletters--sent to constituents on a regular or semi-regular basis.
These provided updates on pending legislation, Baucus’s legislative and
political activities, information on upcoming events, and sometimes
included questionnaires and surveys. All of these groupings are arranged
chronologically, with the exception of Robo Letters. Robo letters, which
date from 1980-1985, are letters generated by an early word-processing
machine known as the “ROBO” system. They consist of carbon copy letters
sent from Baucus to constituents, responding to inquiries and concerns.
Robo Letters from 1980 are arranged in alphabetical order by constituent
last name; letters from 1981-1985 are arranged chronologically.</p>

<p>As is evident in the folder titles, in-office methods of filing
constituent correspondence changed over the years. From 1979-1987,
correspondence is arranged by topics designated by an in-house filing
guide and computer code list. From 1987 through 1998, correspondence is
arranged by unique numerical identifiers assigned to each letter as it
was entered into the office’s Constituent Services System (CSS). These
numbers do not necessarily connote, however, exact chronological order,
and the dates assigned to these folders often correspond to the date on
which correspondence was entered into the CSS, rather than the date on
which a letter was written.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>The second subseries, Petitions, contains mass mailings sent to Max
Baucus between 2010 and 2014. These petitions center on a number of
legislative and topical issues and were mailed by constituents to convey
collective grievances, opinions, and support for/opposition to
particular pieces of legislation. Petitions have been sampled to reduce
volume—original quantities and sampling data are available upon
request.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>The third subseries consists of 799 individual reels of microfilm,
containing filmed images of constituent correspondence and mail reports
generated between 1979 and 1988. Please ask the archivist for a list of
individual reel contents.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries consists of electronic files related to constituent
services, including e-newsletters, mass mailings, scanned correspondence
and microfilm, and a smattering of in-office mail reports exported from
Microsoft Outlook dating from 2011-2013. While much of the scanned
correspondence does likely have analog surrogates, it is not wholly
representative of analog constituent correspondence held within the Max
S. Baucus Papers. Similarly, the microfilm files in this subseries
correspond to a portion of physical microfilm reels, but not all. This
subseries also contains data files exported from Constituent Services
Systems (CSS), large-scale databases that manage the relationship
between a member of Congress and their constituency. These systems
facilitated a variety of activities related to constituent relations,
including scheduling, correspondence and casework management, and
document management. Baucus’s offices utilized a proprietary program
called Intranet Quorum (IQ) from the mid-1990s to 2008, and VOICE from
2008 to 2014.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s legislative work in
the U.S. Senate, encompassing policy research and development, legislation
sponsorship and co-sponsorship, Senate votes, correspondence on legislative
matters, and committee work. It provides a comprehensive overview of
Baucus’s voting habits, relationships with his colleagues in Congress and
other significant stakeholders, legislative priorities, and leadership roles
in the Senate.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains the text of bills and resolutions sponsored or
cosponsored by Baucus, and do not constitute a complete run. All
sponsored and cosponsored bills can be found online at the U.S.
Congress’ website. Legislative Activities are federal compendiums of all
sponsored and cosponsored bills but include summaries of the bills
rather than the full text.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains floor statements, remarks given by Baucus on the
Senate floor. While floor statements are listed here by Congress, they
are also foldered individually by statement topic. Please ask an
archivist for an inventoried list of individual floor statement
topics.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>The Congressional Profile contains material which, as the title implies,
outlines the scope and nature of Baucus’s legislative work while serving
in the Senate. It includes hearing statements, “Dear Colleague” and
other legislative correspondence, briefing books, and legislative
strategy materials. “Dear Colleague” correspondence traditionally
consists of letters sent to and from other members of Congress
requesting support for or against legislation, explaining policy
stances, or urging other legislative actions. The “Dear Colleague”
correspondence in this collection does consist primarily of such
letters, though this category also contains other, less formalized
correspondence between members of Congress, including handwritten and
signed notes and letters on official letterhead. “Dear Official/VIP” is
a Baucus office designation, applied to correspondence to and from
federal officials, ambassadors, executives, and other designated
important administrators. “Dear President” letters consist of letters
sent from Baucus, and sometimes colleagues, to the sitting president
requesting support or urging specific action on executive matters.</p>

<p>Legislative Strategy material contains files laying out legislative plans
and strategies for upcoming Congresses, legislation, or issues. Briefing
books are materials gathered by staffers to prepare Max Baucus for
various trips, public appearances, hearings, meetings, and other events.
They contain background information regarding people he would be meeting
or speaking with, pieces of legislation, companies and cities, answers
to tough questions he might encounter, and any other necessary
preparatory material.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Voting records were produced by four distinct entities: the Congressional
Research Service, the Democratic Policy Committee, various interest
groups, and Baucus’s office itself. While no one grouping contains a
complete run of Baucus’s Senate voting record, together they provide a
comprehensive overview of his voting habits and record. Whereas
materials produced by both the Congressional Research Service and the
Democratic Policy Committee provide a fairly straightforward,
quantitative record of Baucus’s Senate votes, those generated by
interest groups and Baucus staffers tend to be more interpretative.
Interest group voting records examined how Baucus voted in regards to
particular issues, and were often a good gauge of support in various
topical areas. Some voting records produced by Baucus staffers also
focused on particular issues, though they served a variety of other
purposes: tracking Baucus’s support (via votes) of particular
constituencies, bolstering campaign claims, and analyzing his overall
legislative record for future planning purposes. The most recent
document was authored in 2018 by a former staff member, Spencer Gray,
and provides a comprehensive summary of Baucus’s legislative
accomplishments.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Baucus’s service on various congressional committees and subcommittees
during his time in the Senate is the basis for the third subseries,
Committee Files. While official congressional committee records must
remain in the care of the committee itself, the congressional office
records contained within this subseries were generated during work
undertaken by Baucus and his staffers relating to committee
investigations, hearings, legislation, nominations, and other projects.
Significant within this subseries are committee records generated during
Baucus’s tenure as Chairman of the Senate Committee on Finance, in
addition to Senate Committee on Environment and Public Works records
regarding the asbestos environmental disaster in Libby, Montana. Baucus
served as Chairman of the Senate Committee on Finance on three separate
occasions: January 3, 2001-January 20, 2001; June 6, 2001-November 22,
2002; and January 4, 2007-February 6, 2014. He also served as Chairman
of the Committee on Environment and Public Works from January 3,
1993-December 1, 1994.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Subject files consist of materials generated by Baucus and his staffers
during the course of legislative and administrative work. These records,
filed by subject, present perhaps the clearest view of legislative work
in Congress, outlining all aspects of legislative research, development
and implementation. They also illuminate the complex web of relations on
Capitol Hill, outlining the ways in which Baucus and his staff worked
together as well as with other members of Congress, Congressional
staffers, federal employees, public officials, and constituents. Given
the level of detailed description and arrangement, this subseries serves
as a useful tool with which to both trace trending topical issues of the
day and determine legislative priorities for Baucus and his staffers.
Trade, agricultural, and environmental issues are consistently
significant, as are postal services and rural health. File types
include, but are not limited to, memos, correspondence, reports,
publications, legal briefs, invitations, white papers, and legislative
drafts.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Libby files are comprised of materials pertaining to the asbestos-based
environmental disaster which came to light in Libby, Montana in the late
1990s. Max Baucus was instrumental in advocating for the rights of Libby
citizens affected by the asbestos crisis, and pushed for the
Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) to declare a Public Health
Emergency in Libby, the first time that the agency ever did so. He also
cosponsored several pieces of legislation related to asbestos injury
compensation and environmental cleanup. This subseries includes material
related to this legislation as well as the 2005 Department of Justice
criminal proceedings against W.R. Grace and Company, W.R. Grace’s 2007
bankruptcy proceedings, asbestos litigation reform, the EPA
investigation, Libby’s Superfund designation, and asbestos-related
health programs and initiatives.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Documents generated by staffers in the course of their work as
legislative assistants, legislative correspondents, mailroom
specialists, and other legislative and administrative roles in Max
Baucus’s offices constitute the eighth subseries, Staff Files. These
materials focus primarily on particular topical subjects and
legislation, correlating with staffers’ area(s) of expertise. While most
materials were generated in the course of a singular staffer’s work,
some were created by multiple staffers as indicated by initial
inventories accompanying the files.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries consists of electronic records documenting all aspects of
legislative work conducted by Max Baucus and his staffers throughout his
time in the Senate. It contains briefing books, unofficial committee
files, floor statements, hearing statements, legislative correspondence,
memos, and a small selection of sponsored and cosponsored bills, staff
files, and subject files. Of particular note are Senate Committee on
Finance materials, as Baucus was Chairman and/or ranking member for 12
of the 35 total years he served on the Committee. These materials
consist of Finance Chairman letters, press clips, press releases, and
hearing statements. Files also include hearing statements from Baucus’s
other committee work, memos, subject files, voting record materials, and
a number of staff files. Some files and folders have been renamed to
better reflect their contents. While it is likely that many of these
files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact
correlations have not been fully established.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s political campaigns,
shining a light on the complexity of Congressional campaign and polling
activities.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>These files were generated during Max Baucus’s first campaign for public
office in 1972, when he ran for a seat in the Montana House of
Representatives. They contain candidate statements and press materials,
in addition to some campaign ephemera.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries is comprised of materials from Max Baucus’s two House
campaigns in 1974 and 1976, as well as files on campaign opponents and
other candidates. Files include campaign press materials, circulars and
brochures, correspondence and memos.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Materials from Max Baucus’s six campaigns for election to the Senate
constitute the third subseries. The groundwork for Baucus’s political
campaigns was often laid two years in advance, and involved a great
number of campaign staff, volunteers, canvassers, fundraisers, and
political consultants. Files include campaign press materials, circulars
and brochures, memos, debate preparatory materials, voter surveys and
polls, endorsements, election results, and campaign ephemera.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Materials in this subseries consist of audio and video recordings
documenting Baucus’s House and Senate campaigns. Contents include
campaign advertisements, radio spots, interviews, inauguration
activities, campaign events, and debates. A great deal of the magnetic
tape and film reels have little to no accompanying description, and
largely appear to contain short audio and video clips recorded for use
in campaign advertisements. Particularly noteworthy in this subseries
are Baucus family videos from 1955, presumably utilized for campaign
ads.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains results from Max Baucus’s 2000 campaign
primaries, as well as campaign files from Max Baucus’s Senate run in
2002, his fifth Senatorial campaign. These files include campaign media
files, website material, fliers, the opening statement for a July 2002
debate with opponents Mike Taylor, Stan Jones, and Bob Kelleher, and
notes from Taylor’s withdrawal press conference on October 10, 2002.
Some files have been renamed to better reflect their contents.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series consist of materials documenting Max Baucus’s participation in
local and national political initiatives, largely based around his
membership in the Democratic Party. Files relate to Baucus’s earliest
participation in the political process—the 1972 Montana Constitutional
Convention—as well as his involvement in various Democratic Party groups and
activities, including the Democratic Policy Committee and the Democratic
Study Group.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries represents the physical portion of this series, and
contains records relating to Baucus’s participation in local and
national political processes.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries contains a 1972 press clip quoting Max Baucus while he was
serving in the Montana Constitutional Convention. It also contains a

Democratic Policy Committee on April 10, 2003.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series contains photographic material documenting both Max Baucus’s

</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This subseries is comprised of physical photographs and slides spanning
the entirety of Max Baucus’s political career, in addition to some
family and personal photographs. These images document all aspects of
Baucus’s tenure as an elected official, including legislative work,
campaign activities, administrative duties, Democratic Party events, and
public appearances. Many titles encompass multiple images, generally
because multiple photographs were taken at a single event or because
numerous photographs were taken on a single day. Original Baucus
staff-generated titles have been retained.</p>

<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Series I, Frank D. Brown, is divided into six subseries reflecting
the type or theme of the materials included. Subseries 1 includes
correspondence about his mining work and partnerships, a small amount of
personal correspondence mostly with his daughter, Albertina (Tina) Brown, and
letters related to his activities and interest in the Society of Montana
Pioneers. Brown corresponded frequently with James A. Murray, Butte banker,
investor and uncle of James E. Murray; Paul A. Fusz, mine owner; C.D. McClure,
one of the owners of the Granite Mountain and Bi-Metallic Mines; and W.A.
Clark, Montana copper baron. Subseries 2 includes financial records relevant to
Brown's mining businesses, reports on various mines, insurance registers for
his Philipsburg, Montana, based insurance company, and a few legal documents.
Subseries 3 reflects his long time membership in the Society of Montana
Pioneers and his efforts to establish historical monuments and records of
significant events in Montana history. It also includes a copy of the 1865
receipt that served as proof of Brown's arrival in Montana. Subseries 4 holds
Brown's historical writings about Montana, his fiction about forest animals and
also stories set in foreign locales, and speeches about Montana history.
Subseries 5 includes photos of the Brown Family, the Society of Montana
Pioneers, friends and dignatories as well as Philipsburg and Helena locations.
Subseries 6 includes biographical information, news clippings about Brown,
Montana pioneers, and Montana history, a few pieces about the United
Confederate Veterans, and memorabilia.</p>

correspondence related to the Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers. </p>

correspondence related to Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers, insurance and
legal issues.</p>

<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>The collection contains clippings, correspondence, notes, photographs,
publications, slides, audio material, and visual material related to Carling
Malouf’s work and activities in the University of Montana’s Anthropology
Department, as well as Malouf’s research on Native American tribes of the
Northwest and the Plains. Materials include papers related to the history of
the University of Montana’s Department of Anthropology; the history of Kyi-Yo (spelled Kyiyo since 2015),
the University of Montana’s Native American Student Association; and the Native
American Studies Department at the University of Montana. The collection also
contains materials related to the AIM/Wounded Knee incident and ensuing trial,
research on Native American Education, materials related to Montana archaeology
digs and sites, and photographs and notes related to the tribes of Montana.
Also included are papers, correspondence, and notes related to Carling Malouf’s
involvement in the development of the Head Start program, as well as
correspondence, notes, newsletters, and publications related to the Montana
Archaeological Society.</p>
<p> This collection has been divided into five series: Series I:
University Materials (1954-2004), contains notes, departmental budget
information, intradepartmental correspondence, clippings, and publications
related to Carling Malouf’s role in the Department of Anthropology and the
Department of Native American Studies at the University of Montana. Series II:
Subject Files (1891-2006), contains materials related to Carling Maoulf’s
research and interests in archaeology, anthropology, and the Native American
tribes of Montana. The series contains notes, correspondence, clippings, and
publications. The series also contains photographs and negatives, most of which
were not taken by Malouf, but were collections he considered relevant to his
research. Series III: Audio Materials, (ca. 1950-2005 and n.d.), contains
reel-to-reel audio and audiocassettes, A portion of this series is recorded
talks and lectures of Carling Malouf, and recordings of the songs and stories
of Native American tribes of the Northwest. Another portion consists of copies
of prerecorded materials related to the Native Americans in the southwest.
Series IV: Slides, (1939-1975 and n.d.), consists of slides and a slide index.
Some of these images were taken by Carling Malouf and are arranged by tribe
name or by the name of a site of archaeological interest. Others are likely
commercial slides used in the classroom. In 2012 all slides were removed from
carousels and boxes and placed in sleeves. Series V: Moving Image Material, was
added in 2012 because of a large addition of 8mm films to the collection. The
series also includes DVDs and VHS cassettes.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>Notes, departmental budget information, intradepartmental
correspondence, clippings, and publications related to Carling Malouf’s role in
the Department of Anthropology and the Department of Native American Studies at
the University of Montana. </p>

<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">

<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This collection includes both bound and unbound correspondence,
construction and expense reports, financial ledgers, right-of-way descriptions
and maps, activity logs, publicity materials (including photographs and film
reels), and personnel records created by employees of the Northern Pacific
Railway Company and its subsidiary lines between the years 1870 and
1968--though materials are predominantly from the late 1880s through the early
1940s.</p>
<p>The Northern Pacific Railway Records present an extensive and detailed
resource for researching railroad corporate management structures, land
ownership histories for railroad associated properties (particularly in North
Dakota, Montana, Idaho, and Washington state), economic impacts created by
railroad construction and repair, corporate investment into raw material
production for cheap construction supplies (particularly timber), and NPRR
marketing strategies spanning a period of dramatic social and economic change
in the American West. Correspondence records in this collection provide
significant insights into prevailing railroad management structures during the
late 19th and early 20th Centuries--when the rail industry dominated the
social, economic, and political realms of rural American life. As was common
during the time period, NPRR field operation managers were required to be
technical experts as well as administrators. The records generated and/or
compiled by the railroad's engineering staff reveal this complex balance of
designing construction projects, scheduling traffic flows, and managing
day-to-day operations. Rights-of-way documents, property maps, and construction
drawings form a uniquely valuable resource for researching individual real
estate histories and large-scale settlement patterns. The financial records in
this collection provide detailed accounts of occupations and pay schedules for
railroad personnel, the corporation's purchases of construction materials and
sundry supplies (often specific to individual stations), and freight volumes
carried from the region to national markets. The NPRR promotional department
records in this collection preserve sometimes overt and often subtle techniques
intended to both create and reinforce the railroad's preferred images of life
in the Rocky Mountain West and Pacific Northwest. These promotional materials
form a particularly interesting body of evidence for comparison and contrast
with perceptions presented by contemporary regional literatures as well as the
department's focus on highlighting agricultural abundance during periods of
drought and farm foreclosures.</p>
<p>Materials in this collection were predominantly generated or compiled
by engineering and promotions personnel. For the most part, engineering
department recording procedures are consistent; however, the materials in
sub-group 2 were generated by subsidiary and associated rail lines that reflect
widely varrying recording policies and project goals. Additionally, NPRR
engineers tended to move frequently, causing some degree of discontinuity.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This subgroup contains materials generated by employees of the
Engineering Department of the Northern Pacific Railway's primary rail lines.
Series III through XX contain records generated by the geographic divisions the
railway established for hierarchial management (largely defined by state
borders and more localized spurs). Series I and II contain records related to
constructing and managing the primary lines spanning these geographic
divisions. Materials primarily consist of records generated or compiled by NPRR
Engineering Department managers (usually identified as "chief engineer,"
"assistant engineer," or "engineer in charge") regarding property acquistions,
construction projects, repair work, station activities, line traffic,
production numbers from subsidiary operations, and general management.</p>
<p>These materials provide significant primary resources for
examining railroad corporate management structures, land ownership histories
for properties associated with the main railroad lines and spurs, regional and
localized economic impacts created by railroad construction and repair, and
cycles of railroad investment into timber harvesting as a source for cheap
construction materials. Information in this series is primarily presented
through general correspondence, bound financial ledgers, compiled tracking
reports and narrative summaries, blue print diagrams and maps, and day books
maintained by unidentified railroad staff. Throughout this subgroup the phrase
"Force Reports" in folder titles refers to reports filed by line engineers
regarding actual and projected personnel hours (work force) for construction
and repair projects.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series includes materials related to constructing and
managing the NPRR's primary rail line, with particular attention to the Montana
and Idaho legs. This series is further divided into nine subseries highlighting
some of the most intensive construction projects--such as line changes,
tunnels, and re-gradings. Construction planning correspondence, cost estimates,
material requisitions, progress summaries, work force reports, billing records,
rights-of-way documents, and design diagrams predominate the series. These
materials form an extensive record of the railroad's contributions to and
impacts upon the region over nearly 100 years. Construction records provide
detailed accounts of local and imported supplies, work force numbers and pay
schedules, large- and small-scale landscape transformations, and land ownership
patterns.</p>
<p>This series also contains a few daily logs of station activities
and line traffic, providing insights into the railroad's role within rural
communities. Additionally, the series includes several folders of collected
speeches and writings. None of the speeches in this series are attributed to an
author and they appear to be intended for delivery to local civic groups. Most
speeches are accompanied by newspaper articles and pamphlets--apparently
research sources. The speeches and writings sequence includes a few brief
histories of the Northern Pacific line and the communities along its routes.
These histories are written from the perspective of the Northern Pacific (such
that the history of a place tends to begin when the railroad arrived) and
appear to be intended as light reading for passengers on cross-country trips.
The speeches and writings sequence also includes foreign language promotional
materials--mostly Eastern European languages--promoting the agricutlural
abundance of lands along the rail line.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series includes materials related to constructing and
managing the NPRR's second main rail line. As with Series I, materials in this
series exhibit particular attention to the Montana leg of the line; however,
they also include records from the Minnesota and Washington state legs. This
series is further divided into five subseries--the largest of which details
construction projects and traffic reports for the leg between Garrison and
Missoula, Montana. Survey records, construction planning correspondence, cost
estimates, material requisitions, progress summaries, work force reports,
billing records, rights-of-way documents, and design diagrams predominate the
series. Construction records detail local and imported supplies as well as work
force numbers and pay schedules. Correspondence records provide insights into
the challenges engineers faced in designing multiple river crossings due to
narrow canyons, procedures and rationales for subcontracting work projects, and
long-term operations problems (such as derailments and mud/rock slides).</p>
<p>Subseries 2, regarding the Garrison to Missoula leg, includes a
notable sequence of correspondence, encroachment agreements, and construction
designs intended to mitigate the proximity of Northern Pacific Railroad and
Milwaukee Railroad lines through narrow stretches of the Clark Fork River
valley, particularly near Bearmouth and Clinton, Montana. </p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series consists entirely of bound accounting records
maintained by the Main Line Office in Helena, Montana. These records are
statewide expense summaries presented through an account balance format--as
opposed to the project-by-project budgets present in the reports compiled by
field staff.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series includes bound correspondence and financial records
generated by the Chief Engineers of the Idaho Division of the Northern Pacific.
Financial records include detailed reports regarding contruction material
costs, production numbers from the Ellisport (Idaho) lumber mill, passenger
manifests, and station activities--particularly from Wallace, Idaho. These
latter records evidence the economic prominence of Wallace in the Idaho
panhandle prior to the devasting forest fires of 1910 that burned much of the
town and transformed the region's economic patterns.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series consists of correspondence and financial records
generated by the Clarks Fork and Pend d’Oreille Division, operating in western
Montana and northern Idaho. Correspondence materials primarily describe line
repair projects and traffic summaries. Financial records primarily detail
production numbers and shipments (incoming and outgoing) for the NPRR's lumber
yard in Hope, Idaho. Processed lumber from this yard was a significant source
for the railroad's construction projects throughout the region. The absence of
records after 1910 may reflect the impact of the 1910 forest fires that heavily
impacted the region.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series consists of correspondence, financial, and personnel
records generated by the Missoula Division, operating in western Montana and
northern Idaho. Correspondence materials primarily describe line construction
and repair projects as well as traffic summaries. Construction records (both
correspondence and financial journals) detail local and imported supplies as
well as project progress reports. Personnel records in this series provide
insights into the buying habits of railroad employees who used payroll vouchers
to purchase goods from the company (stations and dining cars). In addition,
this series includes a file detailing next-of-kin information for employees at
the Drummond, Montana, station and may serve as a valuable resource for
geneological studies and local history surveys.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series consists of construction correspondence and
financial records generated by the Missouri Division of the NPRR line,
operating primarily in eastern Montana. Correspondence materials primarily
describe line construction and repair projects. Construction records detail
local and imported supplies as well as project progress reports. This series
includes progress journals that provide brief insights into work conditions and
procedural routines at a few construction sites.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series contains one bound volume of clothing, equipment,
and food supply requests from the Assistant Engineer stationed in Harlowton,
Montana--a post within the NPRR's Montana Division.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series consists of correspondence, financial, personnel,
and construction records generated by the Rocky Mountain Division of the NPRR
line. The Rocky Mountain Division spanned most of Montana and this series
includes several distinct and extensive sequences of materials documenting
construction projects, management of multiple branch lines, construction
blueprints, internal and outgoing correspondence, and station histories.
Construction records (both correspondence and financial journals) detail local
and imported supplies as well as project progress reports. Many contruction
records in this series also include blueprint details for site preparation and
design. Branch lines records include Boulder, Bozeman, Camp Creek, Couer
d'Alene, Phillipsburg, Red Bluff, and Ruby Valley. This series contains
extensive blueprints for the branch lines listed above. In addition to the
branch line sequences, this series includes extensive records regarding
redesign and repair of the Missoula to Garrison leg.</p>
<p>Internal correspondence records address construction projects,
company policies, and aquisition procedures. Outgoing correspondence records
predominantly detail rights-of-way inquiries, land acquisition negotiations,
grazing leases for railroad properties, and frieght negotiations with clients.
This sequence (organized by the community or rail line closest to the
recipient) presents extensive documentation of land ownership patterns, grazing
patterns, and agricultural production numbers. Often clients compiled several
years of production numbers into a single report to bolster their requests for
frieght rate adjustments. Station and changing yard reports provide detailed
historical and contemporary accounts of design intentions, community
contributions, renovation projects, cycles of traffic volume and spur
connections, prominent managers, colorful characters associated with the
stations, and generally how the stations fit wihin the context of the larger
community. In addition, financial and personnel records in this series lend
insights into the menus offered by station cafeterias, dining cars, and work
camp cooks.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series includes construction documents generated by the
Yellowstone (River) Division of the Northern Pacific Railroad, operating
primarily in southeastern Montana. These records consist of construction/repair
project correspondence and financial records. Financial records include details
of materials used and projected costs. This series also includes a single
folder of materials regarding the Shields River Branch of the NPRR.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series contains general correspondence, blueprints, and
financial records (including station reports and contracts) from the Washington
and Oregon lines of the NPRR. Correspondence records primarily consist of
intenal documents detailing administrative policies, construction projects,
land purchases, and supply requests as well as extensive exchanges with
construction subcontractors, unaffiliated lines, and station supply vendors.
Within the financial records are abstracts, vouchers, payroll documents, and
correspondence. These financial records compliment the correspondence files in
detailing construction project logistics, personnel actions, and agreements
with subcontractors and vendors. The bound volumes of construction financial
records provide extensive details on materials used, local and imported
resources, work force structures, and projected costs for alternate
approaches.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series contains bound volumes of outgoing correspondence

Washington to western Idaho. Financial records include ledgers, journals, and
other materials that document activities at the Ainsworth Lumber Mill and
construction projects for bridges to Ainsworth, Washington, from Kennewick to
Pasco, Washington, and over the Snake River. The Ainsworth Mill apparently
produced large quanities of timber used for NPRR construction projects but also
marketed to other industrial builders.</p>

<p>This series contains both unbound material and bound volumes
from various construction projects of the Northern Pacific Railway Company as
well as some materials that could not be authoritatively identified with
existing organizational series. The series is divided into ten subseries: (1)
Ainsworth Mill (BC), (2) Anacortes Improvements (Washington), (3) Butte Quarry
(Montana), (4) Columbia River Bridge No. 1, Pasco-Kennewick, (Washington), (5)
J.W. Kendrick: Minnesota/North Dakota/Montana construction, (6) Lewistown
Outside Yards (Idaho), (7) Seattle Extension, (8) Seattle Wharf Work, (9)
Superior Ore Dock (Wisconsin), and (10) Unidentified. Materials in this series
include outgoing correspondence primarily documenting construction projects
(subcontractor and vendor agreements) and transportation passes. Financial
records in this series generally present construction estimates and actual
expenses, cash books, and activity logs.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent>
<p>This series contains photographs intended as a documentary
record for railway operations--such as construction projects, railroad
facilities, engines, and work conditions--though some promotional photographs
are present as well. Photographs in this series constitute a valuable resource
documenting landscapes, towns, community events, rural life, work crews, rail
infrastructures, and weather events throughout the high plains and montane
valleys of the central and western United States--as well as spanning eighty
years of dramatic physical, economic, and social change. Materials in this
series exhibit several distinct sequences of image themes. Among these are
extensive Montana bridge, station, and change yard sequences (often identified
by location or structural number but seldom both). Billings, Bozeman, Brockway,
Circle, Glendive, Lindsay, and Missoula, Montana--and their respective outlying
areas--are among the communities most significantly documented by the images in
this series.</p>
<p>Another extensive image sequence documents the possessions,
staple crops, and vistas of Mission Valley (Montana) homestead families during
the late 1920s. The Mission Valley photographs were captured for promotional
purposes, as opposed to the construction and facility images that dominate this
series. Another promotional image sequence includes produce being carried to
market. These promotional photographs were primarily intended to convey
perceptions of abundance and help the railroad sell rail accessible farm
plots.</p>
<p>The majority of materials in this series are identified and
described individually, with reference to location and date whenever such
information could be established; however, the series also includes some
grouped photographs recovered from a separated collection. Photographs
individually identified and described during original processing were assigned
identification numbers according to the year and order in which they were
processed. During reprocessing all these photographs were assigned new
reference numbers to reflect their relationship within the larger
collection--regardless of year--though the original numbers are noted in
brackets for cross-reference. </p>
<p>Photographs in boxes 396, 397, and OS1 are not individually
processed. These materials are grouped by subject following the titles imposed
upon them after they were seperated from the original collection and before
they were donated to the K. Ross Toole Archives. The conditions and purposes
under which these materials were originally separated are unknown. </p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent>
<p>This subgroup contains materials generated by employees of the
branch, predecessor, and subsidiary lines associated with the Northern Pacific
Railway Company. These materials were primarily generated by Engineering
Department personnel and descriptive terminology is generally consistent with
that of Subgroup 1. This subgroup consists of 37 series--most of which are
essentially small fragments of the unit's original operations records.
Materials include property acquistion correspondence, construction project
records (correspondence, financial, and legal records), progress reports, line
traffic summaries, and personnel documents.</p>
<p>These materials provide significant primary resources for
examining railroad corporate management structures, land ownership histories
for properties associated with branch and subsidiary lines as well as regional
and localized economic impacts created by railroad construction and repair.
Information in this series is primarily presented through general
correspondence, bound financial ledgers, compiled tracking reports and
narrative summaries, blue print diagrams and maps, and day books maintained by
unidentified railroad staff. Throughout this subgroup the phrase "Force
Reports" in folder titles refers to reports filed by line engineers regarding
actual and projected personnel hours (work force) for construction and repair
projects.</p>
<p>Historical and contextual information for some of the branch,
predecessor, and related rail lines in this subgroup is very limited. Whenever
possible, the scope and notes for each series identify the geographic location
of the lines and dates for incoporation into the NPRR system.</p>
</scopecontent>

<p>The Clearwater Short Line Railway Company's main lines were from

The company was incorporated on November 9, 1898, and sold to the Northern

line, Lapwai Branch to Lewiston, Potlatch Branch, and Lolo Branch. General
correspondence materials include telegrams, rights of way, construction

<scopecontent>
<p>This subgroup consists of film and photographs produced by the
Advertising and Publicity Department of the Northern Pacific Railroad. These
materials document the landscapes and communities along the primary railway
routes and the promotional techniques employed by the railway company to
encourage both tourism and settlement in the region. Additionally, materials in
this subgroup provide interesting primary sources for comparing and contrasting
the perceptions of life in the Rocky Mountain West and Pacfic Northwest
promoted by the railroad versus those propagated by news media, agricultural
agents, and regional authors during the first half of the 20th Century.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent>
<p>This series contains films produced by the railroad promotions
department targeted for Eastern and Midwestern U.S. populations and intended to
promote rail travel as the best method for viewing the physical beauty and
cultural legacy of the Rocky Mountain West and Pacific Northwest. Many of the
films portray the region as retaining the character of the "Old West" while
incorporating the conveniences of modern life. Materials in this series provide
insights into the changes and continuities of railroad advertising techniques
spanning the 1920s through the early 1940s--particularly the employment of
American Indian greeters, staging tour events at national parks and locations
of historical interest, and the promotion of company-owned dude ranches.
Additionally, these films preserve images of Rocky Mountain and Pacific
Northwest landscapes before the industrial and housing development booms of the
post-World War II period.</p>
<p>This series contains duplicate film reels for 128(LX):7,
128(LX):13, and 128(LX):18. Reels 128(LX):1, 128(LX):2, 128(LX):7, 128(LX):10,
and 128(LX):20 are color film. Only reel 128(LX):14 has sound. Reel 128(LX):15
is a copy of the original at the Oregon Historical Society.</p>
<p>All collection VHS tapes are duplicates of film reels. For each
instance of a one-to-one relationship, tapes were assigned a common reference
number to the film reel original. These tapes correspond with film reels
128(LX):11, 128(LX):12, and 128(LX):13. One VHS tape contains transfers of
several film reels. In this one instance the VHS tape was assigned a unique
reference number, 128(LX):21.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This series includes photographs generated by the promotions
department of the railway. These photographs appear to have been intended for
promotional literature, particularly to highlight the agricultural potential of
the high plains and river valleys along Northern Pacific Railway routes. The
majority of these photographs were captured after the first major homesteading
wave in the region (1890s through 1918) and during the disastorous cycles of
high plains droughts that resulted in farm foreclosures and homesteader
emmigration (1919 through 1933). These photographs depicting agricultural
abundance and opportunity present images in marked contrast to the tenor of
agricultural studies and regional news reports during the period.</p>
<p>In addition, the photographs in this series present images of
the towns and landscapes along the railway routes during the early stages of
industrial production in the region--when the impact of railroad access is
evidenced by transformations in agricultural and timber harvesting methods as
well as the capacity of transporting high volume goods to distant markets.
Though this series contains photographs spanning the Northern Pacific line, the
images predominantly depict the farms and rail hub towns of Montana and Idaho
during the 1920s and 1930s. Also included are some documentary images of
Northern Pacific promotional exhibits which help to reveal additional context
for how the department used the other photographs in this series.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>Photographs of St. Ignatius Mission in St. Ignatius, Montana, church
services, Father Louis Taelman [ordained in 1898, Father Louis Taelman spent
years with the Crow, Spokane and Kalsipell Indian tribes as well as serving as
president of Gonzaga University; he spent most of his life with the Flathead
Indians at St. Ignatius], Father John J. Brown, and Native American
parishioners including Chief Eneas Granjo, Peter Beaverhead, Chief Paul Charlo,
and Joe Woodcock.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="520">
<p>This collection contains correspondence, financial, legal,
promotional, personal, and photographic records relating to the McGowan
Commercial Company of Plains, Montana. Financial records comprise the majority
of collection items. These materials provide insights into the challenges and
successes of operating a rural general merchandising business as well as the
early economic history of Plains, Montana.</p>
</scopecontent>
<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">

<p>This series contains some records and memorabilia from James A.
McGowan and Clifford H. Rittenour. Materials include an unsigned, undated draft
copy of James A. McGowan's will. Clifford H. Rittenour was a manager of McGowan
Commercial Company and became President after James McGowan's death. These
items include records and memorabilia from Rittenour's student days at the
University of Montana, Missoula.</p>

<scopecontent encodinganalog="5202_">
<p>This series includes fifteen photographs. Most of the photographs
depict Plains, Montana, particularly the McGowan Mercantile Company store and
hotel. Also included are five photographs documenting the procession to James
A. McGowan's funeral, two photographs of Mr. and Mrs. Clifford H. Rittenour's

<scopecontent>
<p>This series contains a letter written to Rex Blazer and John
Wachsmuth on May 20, 1977, by Robert W. Williams, forest supervisor for the
Beaverhead National Forest. Williams notified them that the public comment
period on the forest's management plan and draft environmental impact statement
was extended. Blazer and Wachsmuth respond by urging Williams to consider
cultural resources, like mining camps, when creating a forest plan. They tell
Williams about the artifacts located in Beaverhead National Forest. They assert
that the U.S. Forest Service would be violating a number of laws if they
continued to ignore the significance of these historic sites.</p>

<p>This collection includes two deeds for the exchange of land in
Montana. The first deed, dated March 24, 1899, involves the sale of $4,007.98
worth of land in what was then called Deer Lodge County. The Northern Pacific
Railway Company deeded the land to John W. Blair. The second deed, dated
January 27, 1902, involves the sale of $3,000 worth of land in Powell County.
Mary E. Mitchell, J. Paul Mitchell, William D. Mitchell and Mary M. Scanland
deeded the land to Conrad Kohrs. Kohrs was called the “cattle king of Montana”
and owned a 30,000-acre ranch near the city of Deer Lodge. He owned a large
store in the 1860s at Last Chance Gulch, where Blair mined, that mostly sold
beef to miners. </p>

<p>The Forest Service District 1 flume manual is a bound typescript
containing four chapters, including a chapter on the advantages and
disadvantages of flumes (a means of floating logs down mountainous terrain to
sawmills or shipping points) by Supervisor Dorr Skeels with assistance from
District Engineer J. P. Martin; a chapter on process of choosing locations for
flumes and constructing them by E.M. Bonner, chief of Geography; data on
hydrography by District Engineer E.W. Kramer with tables on stream flow by J.P.
Martin; and blueprints on the construction of flumes by W.D. Starbird. E.W.
Kramer also offered working examples in his report on two existing flumes, the
Allen Flume near Anaconda, Montana, and Rochet Flume near St. Joe, Idaho. The
manual is illustrated with original photographs. </p>

experiences in Vietnam. The reminiscence includes a description of airmobile
combat engineer unit projects and daily life, in addition to original poetry by

and an after action report. The second is a revised and spiral bound version of the reminiscence entitled "Vietnam Journal, 1969-1970."</p>

Stars and Stripes" Newspaper. Many articles have a connection to the O'Reilly

articles are undated, but appear to be arranged in chronological order.</p>

marked. There are also H. David Cogley's personal documents, including a

report.</p>

Participation Program which Brandborg helped develop and implement in the late
